<li>Bottom: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Hale–Bopp">Hale–Bopp</a> seen from Earth in 1997, and <a href="/wiki/C/2011_W3_(Lovejoy)" title="C/2011 W3 (Lovejoy)">C/2011 W3 (Lovejoy)</a> imaged from Earth orbit</li></ul></div></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>comet</b> is an icy, <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System body</a> that, when passing close to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, warms and begins to release gases, a process that is called <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a>. This produces a visible atmosphere or <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>, and sometimes also a <a href="/wiki/Comet_tail" title="Comet tail">tail</a>. These phenomena are due to the effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> acting upon the nucleus of the comet. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_nuclei" title="Comet nuclei">Comet nuclei</a> range from a few hundred meters to tens of kilometers across and are composed of loose collections of ice, dust, and small rocky particles. The coma may be up to 15 times Earth's diameter, while the tail may stretch beyond one <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a>. If sufficiently bright, a comet may be seen from Earth without the aid of a telescope and may <a href="/wiki/Subtended_angle" title="Subtended angle">subtend</a> an arc of 30° (60 Moons) across the sky. Comets have been observed and recorded since ancient times by many cultures and religions.
</p><p>Comets usually have highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> elliptical orbits, and they have a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital periods</a>, ranging from several years to potentially several millions of years. <a href="#Short_period">Short-period comets</a> originate in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> or its associated <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, which lie beyond the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. <a href="#Long_period">Long-period comets</a> are thought to originate in the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, a spherical cloud of icy bodies extending from outside the Kuiper belt to halfway to the nearest star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Long-period comets are set in motion towards the Sun from the Oort cloud by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_perturbation" title="Gravitational perturbation">gravitational perturbations</a> caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars#Future_and_past" title="List of nearest stars">passing stars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">galactic tide</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_comet" title="Hyperbolic comet">Hyperbolic comets</a> may pass once through the inner Solar System before being flung to interstellar space. The appearance of a comet is called an apparition.
</p><p>Comets are distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> by the presence of an extended, gravitationally unbound atmosphere surrounding their central nucleus. This atmosphere has parts termed the coma (the central part immediately surrounding the nucleus) and the tail (a typically linear section consisting of dust or gas blown out from the coma by the Sun's light pressure or outstreaming solar wind plasma). However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct_comets" title="Extinct comets">extinct comets</a> that have passed close to the Sun many times have lost nearly all of their <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> ices and dust and may come to resemble small asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Asteroids are thought to have a different origin from comets, having formed inside the orbit of Jupiter rather than in the outer Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">main-belt comets</a> and active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaur</a> minor planets has blurred the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid#Terminology" title="Asteroid">distinction between asteroids and comets</a>. In the early 21st century, the discovery of some minor bodies with long-period comet orbits, but characteristics of inner solar system asteroids, were called <a href="/wiki/Manx_comet" title="Manx comet">Manx comets</a>. They are still classified as comets, such as C/2014 S3 (PANSTARRS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> 27 Manx comets were found from 2013 to 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of November 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there are 4584 known comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> However, this represents only a tiny fraction of the total potential comet population, as the reservoir of comet-like bodies in the outer Solar System (in the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>) is estimated to be one trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erickson2003_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erickson2003-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Couper2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Couper2014-9">[9]</a></sup> Roughly one comet per year is visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, though many of those are faint and unspectacular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Particularly bright examples are called "<a href="/wiki/Great_comet" title="Great comet">great comets</a>". Comets have been visited by unmanned probes such as the European Space Agency's <i><a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">Rosetta</a></i>, which became the first to land a robotic spacecraft on a comet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philae_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philae-11">[11]</a></sup> and NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i>, which blasted a crater on Comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> to study its interior.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI,_folio_10v).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="117" data-file-width="401" decoding="async" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI%2C_folio_10v%29.jpg/220px-Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI%2C_folio_10v%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI%2C_folio_10v%29.jpg/330px-Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI%2C_folio_10v%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI%2C_folio_10v%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle_-_cometa_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Tiberius_A_VI,_folio_10v).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comet was mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> that allegedly made an appearance in 729 AD.</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>comet</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">cometa</i></span> from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">comēta</i></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">comētēs</i></span>. That, in turn, is a <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">romanization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">κομήτης</span></span> 'wearing long hair', and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> notes that the term (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀστὴρ</span></span>) <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">κομήτης</span></span> already meant 'long-haired star, comet' in Greek. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Κομήτης</span></span> was derived from <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">κομᾶν</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">koman</i></span>) 'to wear the hair long', which was itself derived from <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">κόμη</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">komē</i></span>) 'the hair of the head' and was used to mean 'the tail of a comet'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> for comets (represented in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>) is <span class="nowrap"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">U+2604</span> </span><span style="font-size:125%;line-height:1em">☄</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">COMET</span>, consisting of a small disc with three hairlike extensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Physical_Structure.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Comet_Physical_Structure.svg/300px-Comet_Physical_Structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Comet_Physical_Structure.svg/450px-Comet_Physical_Structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Comet_Physical_Structure.svg/600px-Comet_Physical_Structure.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Physical_Structure.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing the physical characteristics of a comet:<br/>a) Nucleus, b) Coma, c) Gas/ion tail d) Dust tail, e) Hydrogen envelope, f) Orbital velocity direction, g) Direction to the Sun.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleus">Nucleus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nucleus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hartley_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1171" data-file-width="1683" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/220px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/330px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/440px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hartley_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nucleus of <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">103P/Hartley</a> as imaged during a spacecraft flyby. The nucleus is about 2 km in length.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comet_nucleus" title="Comet nucleus">Comet nucleus</a></div>
<p>The solid, core structure of a comet is known as the nucleus. Cometary nuclei are composed of an amalgamation of <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comet_dust" title="Comet dust">dust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a>, and frozen <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> As such, they are popularly described as "dirty snowballs" after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Whipple" title="Fred Whipple">Fred Whipple</a>'s model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Comets with a higher dust content have been called "icy dirtballs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The term "icy dirtballs" arose after observation of <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Comet 9P/Tempel 1</a> collision with an "impactor" probe sent by NASA Deep Impact mission in July 2005. Research conducted in 2014 suggests that comets are like "<a href="/wiki/Fried_ice_cream" title="Fried ice cream">deep fried ice cream</a>", in that their surfaces are formed of dense crystalline ice mixed with <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, while the interior ice is colder and less dense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150210_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150210-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The surface of the nucleus is generally dry, dusty or rocky, suggesting that the ices are hidden beneath a surface crust several metres thick. In addition to the gases already mentioned, the nuclei contain a variety of organic compounds, which may include <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, and perhaps more complex molecules such as long-chain <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In 2009, it was confirmed that the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> had been found in the comet dust recovered by NASA's <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In August 2011, a report, based on <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> studies of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> found on Earth, was published suggesting <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> components (<a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>, and related organic molecules) may have been formed on <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callahan_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callahan-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steigerwald_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steigerwald-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_borrelly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Comet_borrelly.jpg/220px-Comet_borrelly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Comet_borrelly.jpg/330px-Comet_borrelly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Comet_borrelly.jpg/440px-Comet_borrelly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_borrelly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/19P/Borrelly" title="19P/Borrelly">Comet Borrelly</a> exhibits jets, but has no surface ice.</div></div></div>
<p>The outer surfaces of cometary nuclei have a very low <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, making them among the least reflective objects found in the Solar System. The <a href="/wiki/Giotto_(spacecraft)" title="Giotto (spacecraft)">Giotto</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> found that the nucleus of <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a> (1P/Halley) reflects about four percent of the light that falls on it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dark_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-dark-24">[24]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_1" title="Deep Space 1">Deep Space 1</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/19P/Borrelly" title="19P/Borrelly">Comet Borrelly</a>'s surface reflects less than 3.0%;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dark_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-dark-24">[24]</a></sup> by comparison, <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> reflects seven percent. The dark surface material of the nucleus may consist of complex organic compounds. Solar heating drives off lighter <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>, leaving behind larger organic compounds that tend to be very dark, like <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">crude oil</a>. The low reflectivity of cometary surfaces causes them to absorb the heat that drives their <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comet nuclei with radii of up to 30 kilometers (19 mi) have been observed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> but ascertaining their exact size is difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The nucleus of <a href="/wiki/322P/SOHO" title="322P/SOHO">322P/SOHO</a> is probably only 100–200 meters (330–660 ft) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soho1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-soho1-28">[28]</a></sup> A lack of smaller comets being detected despite the increased sensitivity of instruments has led some to suggest that there is a real lack of comets smaller than 100 meters (330 ft) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Known comets have been estimated to have an average density of 0.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup> (0.35 oz/cu in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britt2006-30">[30]</a></sup> Because of their low mass, comet nuclei do not <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">become spherical</a> under their own <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> and therefore have irregular shapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_wild_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Comet_wild_2.jpg/220px-Comet_wild_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Comet_wild_2.jpg/330px-Comet_wild_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Comet_wild_2.jpg/440px-Comet_wild_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_wild_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comet <a href="/wiki/81P/Wild" title="81P/Wild">81P/Wild</a> exhibits jets on light side and dark side, stark relief, and is dry.</div></div></div>
<p>Roughly six percent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroids</a> are thought to be the <a href="/wiki/Extinct_comet" title="Extinct comet">extinct nuclei of comets</a> that no longer experience outgassing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dormant_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-dormant-32">[32]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/14827_Hypnos" title="14827 Hypnos">14827 Hypnos</a> and <a href="/wiki/3552_Don_Quixote" title="3552 Don Quixote">3552 Don Quixote</a>.
</p><p>Results from the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)"><i>Rosetta</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Philae_(spacecraft)" title="Philae (spacecraft)"><i>Philae</i></a> spacecraft show that the nucleus of <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov–Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov–Gerasimenko</a> has no magnetic field, which suggests that magnetism may not have played a role in the early formation of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20150414_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20150414-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20150414_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20150414-34">[34]</a></sup> Further, the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)#Instruments" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">ALICE spectrograph</a> on <i>Rosetta</i> determined that <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> (within 1 km (0.62 mi) above the <a href="/wiki/Comet_nucleus" title="Comet nucleus">comet nucleus</a>) produced from <a href="/wiki/Photoionization" title="Photoionization">photoionization</a> of water molecules by <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">solar radiation</a>, and not <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> from the Sun as thought earlier, are responsible for the degradation of water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> released from the comet nucleus into its coma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150602_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150602-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA-20150602_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-20150602-36">[36]</a></sup> Instruments on the <i>Philae</i> lander found at least sixteen organic compounds at the comet's surface, four of which (<a href="/wiki/Acetamide" title="Acetamide">acetamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methyl_isocyanate" title="Methyl isocyanate">methyl isocyanate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propionaldehyde" title="Propionaldehyde">propionaldehyde</a>) have been detected for the first time on a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo20150730_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo20150730-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa20150730_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa20150730-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20150731_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20150731-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coma">Coma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Coma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">Coma (cometary)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3912" data-file-width="3925" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg/220px-Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg/330px-Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg/440px-Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble%27s_Last_Look_at_Comet_ISON_Before_Perihelion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a> image of <a href="/wiki/Comet_ISON" title="Comet ISON">Comet ISON</a> shortly before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The streams of dust and gas thus released form a huge and extremely thin atmosphere around the comet called the "coma". The force exerted on the coma by the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> cause an enormous "tail" to form pointing away from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The coma is generally made of water and dust, with water making up to 90% of the <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a> that outflow from the nucleus when the comet is within 3 to 4 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (450,000,000 to 600,000,000 km; 280,000,000 to 370,000,000 mi) of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Combi2004_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Combi2004-49">[49]</a></sup> The <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1060974363">.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su{display:inline-block;font-size:80%;line-height:1;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su>span{display:block}.mw-parser-output sub.template-chem2-sub{font-size:80%;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output sup.template-chem2-sup{font-size:80%;vertical-align:0.65em}</style><span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>O</a></span> parent molecule is destroyed primarily through <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">photodissociation</a> and to a much smaller extent <a href="/wiki/Photoionization" title="Photoionization">photoionization</a>, with the solar wind playing a minor role in the destruction of water compared to <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Combi2004_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Combi2004-49">[49]</a></sup> Larger dust particles are left along the comet's orbital path whereas smaller particles are pushed away from the Sun into the comet's tail by <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">light pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the solid nucleus of comets is generally less than 60 kilometers (37 mi) across, the coma may be thousands or millions of kilometers across, sometimes becoming larger than the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> For example, about a month after an outburst in October 2007, comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/17P/Holmes" title="17P/Holmes">17P/Holmes</a> briefly had a tenuous dust atmosphere larger than the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atmosphere2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-atmosphere2-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1811" title="Great Comet of 1811">Great Comet of 1811</a> also had a coma roughly the diameter of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-primer_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-primer-53">[53]</a></sup> Even though the coma can become quite large, its size can decrease about the time it crosses the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> around 1.5 astronomical units (220,000,000 km; 140,000,000 mi) from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-primer_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-primer-53">[53]</a></sup> At this distance the solar wind becomes strong enough to blow the gas and dust away from the coma, and in doing so enlarging the tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-primer_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-primer-53">[53]</a></sup> Ion tails have been observed to extend one astronomical unit (150 million km) or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atmosphere2_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-atmosphere2-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="747" data-file-width="1065" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg/220px-PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg/330px-PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg/440px-PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20119-CometChristensen-C2006W3-CO2-WISE-20100420.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>C/2006 W3 (Chistensen) emitting carbon gas (IR image)</div></div></div>
<p>Both the coma and tail are illuminated by the Sun and may become visible when a comet passes through the inner Solar System, the dust reflects sunlight directly while the gases glow from <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionisation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-le_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-le-54">[54]</a></sup> Most comets are too faint to be visible without the aid of a <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, but a few each decade become bright enough to be visible to the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Occasionally a comet may experience a huge and sudden outburst of gas and dust, during which the size of the coma greatly increases for a period of time. This happened in 2007 to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/17P/Holmes" title="17P/Holmes">Comet Holmes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atmosphere1_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-atmosphere1-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, comets were found to emit <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> This greatly surprised astronomers because X-ray emission is usually associated with very <a href="/wiki/Black-body_radiation" title="Black-body radiation">high-temperature bodies</a>. The X-rays are generated by the interaction between comets and the solar wind: when highly charged solar wind ions fly through a cometary atmosphere, they collide with cometary atoms and molecules, "stealing" one or more electrons from the atom in a process called "charge exchange". This exchange or transfer of an electron to the solar wind ion is followed by its de-excitation into the ground state of the ion by the emission of X-rays and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_ultraviolet" title="Far ultraviolet">far ultraviolet</a> photons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bow_shock">Bow shock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bow shock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">Bow shocks</a> form as a result of the interaction between the solar wind and the cometary ionosphere, which is created by the ionization of gases in the coma. As the comet approaches the Sun, increasing outgassing rates cause the coma to expand, and the sunlight ionizes gases in the coma. When the solar wind passes through this ion coma, the bow shock appears.
</p><p>The first observations were made in the 1980s and 1990s as several spacecraft flew by comets <a href="/wiki/21P/Giacobini%E2%80%93Zinner" title="21P/Giacobini–Zinner">21P/Giacobini–Zinner</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> 1P/Halley,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/26P/Grigg%E2%80%93Skjellerup" title="26P/Grigg–Skjellerup">26P/Grigg–Skjellerup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It was then found that the bow shocks at comets are wider and more gradual than the sharp planetary bow shocks seen at, for example, Earth. These observations were all made near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> when the bow shocks already were fully developed.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)"><i>Rosetta</i></a> spacecraft observed the bow shock at comet <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov–Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov–Gerasimenko</a> at an early stage of bow shock development when the outgassing increased during the comet's journey toward the Sun. This young bow shock was called the "infant bow shock". The infant bow shock is asymmetric and, relative to the distance to the nucleus, wider than fully developed bow shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tails">Tails</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tails">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comet_tail" title="Comet tail">Comet tail</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cometorbit01.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="623" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cometorbit01.svg/280px-Cometorbit01.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cometorbit01.svg/420px-Cometorbit01.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cometorbit01.svg/560px-Cometorbit01.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cometorbit01.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical direction of tails during a comet's orbit near the Sun</div></div></div>
<p>In the outer <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, comets remain frozen and inactive and are extremely difficult or impossible to detect from Earth due to their small size. Statistical detections of inactive comet nuclei in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> have been reported from observations by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochran1995_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochran1995-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochran1998_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochran1998-64">[64]</a></sup> but these detections have been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown1997_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1997-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt1996_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt1996-66">[66]</a></sup> As a comet approaches the inner Solar System, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> causes the volatile materials within the comet to vaporize and stream out of the nucleus, carrying dust away with them.
</p><p>The streams of dust and gas each form their own distinct tail, pointing in slightly different directions. The tail of dust is left behind in the comet's orbit in such a manner that it often forms a curved tail called the type II or dust tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-le_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-le-54">[54]</a></sup> At the same time, the ion or type I tail, made of gases, always points directly away from the Sun because this gas is more strongly affected by the solar wind than is dust, following magnetic field lines rather than an orbital trajectory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> On occasions—such as when Earth passes through a comet's orbital plane, the <a href="/wiki/Antitail" title="Antitail">antitail</a>, pointing in the opposite direction to the ion and dust tails, may be seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Parts.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2679" data-file-width="1942" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Comet_Parts.svg/170px-Comet_Parts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Comet_Parts.svg/255px-Comet_Parts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Comet_Parts.svg/340px-Comet_Parts.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Parts.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of a comet showing the <a href="/wiki/Antitail" title="Antitail">dust trail</a>, the dust tail, and the ion gas tail formed by <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The observation of antitails contributed significantly to the discovery of solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The ion tail is formed as a result of the ionization by solar ultra-violet radiation of particles in the coma. Once the particles have been ionized, they attain a net positive electrical charge, which in turn gives rise to an "induced <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>" around the comet. The comet and its induced magnetic field form an obstacle to outward flowing solar wind particles. Because the relative orbital speed of the comet and the solar wind is supersonic, a <a href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a> is formed upstream of the comet in the flow direction of the solar wind. In this bow shock, large concentrations of cometary ions (called "pick-up ions") congregate and act to "load" the solar magnetic field with plasma, such that the field lines "drape" around the comet forming the ion tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pp_864_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pp_864-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the ion tail loading is sufficient, the magnetic field lines are squeezed together to the point where, at some distance along the ion tail, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">magnetic reconnection</a> occurs. This leads to a "tail disconnection event".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pp_864_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-pp_864-70">[70]</a></sup> This has been observed on a number of occasions, one notable event being recorded on 20 April 2007, when the ion tail of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encke%27s_Comet" title="Encke's Comet">Encke's Comet</a> was completely severed while the comet passed through a <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejection</a>. This event was observed by the <a href="/wiki/STEREO" title="STEREO">STEREO space probe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> scientists reported that the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> of the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> streams outwards in a manner similar to the ion tail seen streaming from a comet under similar conditions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-20130129_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-20130129-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130130_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130130-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jets">Jets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2220" data-file-width="2047" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg/170px-Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg/255px-Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg/340px-Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hartley2jets2_epoxi_big.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gas and snow jets of <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">103P/Hartley</a></div></div></div>
<p>Uneven heating can cause newly generated gases to break out of a weak spot on the surface of comet's nucleus, like a geyser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jets_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-jets-74">[74]</a></sup> These streams of gas and dust can cause the nucleus to spin, and even split apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jets_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-jets-74">[74]</a></sup> In 2010 it was revealed <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> (frozen carbon dioxide) can power jets of material flowing out of a comet nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Infrared imaging of Hartley 2 shows such jets exiting and carrying with it dust grains into the coma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_characteristics">Orbital characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Orbital characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most comets are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_bodies" title="Small Solar System bodies">small Solar System bodies</a> with elongated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_orbit" title="Elliptical orbit">elliptical orbits</a> that take them close to the Sun for a part of their orbit and then out into the further reaches of the Solar System for the remainder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Comets are often classified according to the length of their <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital periods</a>: The longer the period the more elongated the ellipse.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_period"><span class="anchor" id="Short-period_comet"></span> Short period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Short period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_numbered_comets" title="List of numbered comets">List of numbered comets</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Halley-type_comets" title="List of Halley-type comets">List of Halley-type comets</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_periodic_comets" title="List of periodic comets">Periodic comets</a> or short-period comets are generally defined as those having orbital periods of less than 200 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> They usually orbit more-or-less in the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> plane in the same direction as the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Their orbits typically take them out to the region of the outer planets (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and beyond) at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a>; for example, the aphelion of Halley's Comet is a little beyond the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. Comets whose aphelia are near a major planet's orbit are called its "family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson1909_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson1909-80">[80]</a></sup> Such families are thought to arise from the planet capturing formerly long-period comets into shorter orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the shorter orbital period extreme, Encke's Comet has an orbit that does not reach the orbit of Jupiter, and is known as an <b>Encke-type comet</b>. Short-period comets with orbital periods less than 20 years and low inclinations (up to 30 degrees) to the ecliptic are called traditional <b>Jupiter-family comets</b> (JFCs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britastro_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-britastro-83">[83]</a></sup> Those like Halley, with orbital periods of between 20 and 200 years and inclinations extending from zero to more than 90 degrees, are called <b>Halley-type comets</b> (HTCs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2006_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2006-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewitt2002_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-jewitt2002-85">[85]</a></sup> As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 94 HTCs have been observed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTC_JPL_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-HTC_JPL-86">[86]</a></sup> compared with 744 identified JFCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yfernandez_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-yfernandez-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recently discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-belt_comets" title="Main-belt comets">main-belt comets</a> form a distinct class, orbiting in more circular orbits within the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because their elliptical orbits frequently take them close to the giant planets, comets are subject to further <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">gravitational perturbations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Short-period comets have a tendency for their aphelia to coincide with a <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planet</a>'s semi-major axis, with the JFCs being the largest group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britastro_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-britastro-83">[83]</a></sup> It is clear that comets coming in from the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> often have their orbits strongly influenced by the gravity of giant planets as a result of a close encounter. Jupiter is the source of the greatest perturbations, being more than twice as massive as all the other planets combined. These perturbations can deflect long-period comets into shorter orbital periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on their orbital characteristics, short-period comets are thought to originate from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">centaurs</a> and the Kuiper belt/<a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> —a disk of objects in the trans-Neptunian region—whereas the source of long-period comets is thought to be the far more distant spherical Oort cloud (after the Dutch astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_Hendrik_Oort" title="Jan Hendrik Oort">Jan Hendrik Oort</a> who hypothesized its existence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Vast swarms of comet-like bodies are thought to orbit the Sun in these distant regions in roughly circular orbits. Occasionally the gravitational influence of the outer planets (in the case of Kuiper belt objects) or nearby stars (in the case of Oort cloud objects) may throw one of these bodies into an elliptical orbit that takes it inwards toward the Sun to form a visible comet. Unlike the return of periodic comets, whose orbits have been established by previous observations, the appearance of new comets by this mechanism is unpredictable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> When flung into the orbit of the sun, and being continuously dragged towards it, tons of matter are stripped from the comets which greatly influence their lifetime; the more stripped, the shorter they live and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_period">Long period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Long period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_long-period_comets" title="List of long-period comets">List of long-period comets</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_near-parabolic_comets" title="List of near-parabolic comets">List of near-parabolic comets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_hyperbolic_comets" title="List of hyperbolic comets">List of hyperbolic comets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="546" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg/220px-Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg/330px-Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg/440px-Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Kohoutek_orbit_p391.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Orbits of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Kohoutek" title="Comet Kohoutek">Comet Kohoutek</a> (red) and Earth (blue), illustrating the high <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of its orbit and its rapid motion when close to the Sun.</div></div></div>
<p>Long-period comets have highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> orbits and periods ranging from 200 years to thousands or even millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBP_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBP-96">[96]</a></sup> An eccentricity greater than 1 when near perihelion does not necessarily mean that a comet will leave the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elenin2011_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elenin2011-97">[97]</a></sup> For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2006_P1" title="C/2006 P1">Comet McNaught</a> had a heliocentric osculating eccentricity of 1.000019 near its perihelion passage <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">epoch</a> in January 2007 but is bound to the Sun with roughly a 92,600-year orbit because the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> drops below 1 as it moves farther from the Sun. The future orbit of a long-period comet is properly obtained when the <a href="/wiki/Osculating_orbit" title="Osculating orbit">osculating orbit</a> is computed at an epoch after leaving the planetary region and is calculated with respect to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentric_coordinates_(astronomy)" title="Barycentric coordinates (astronomy)">center of mass of the Solar System</a>. By definition long-period comets remain gravitationally bound to the Sun; those comets that are ejected from the Solar System due to close passes by major planets are no longer properly considered as having "periods". The orbits of long-period comets take them far beyond the outer planets at aphelia, and the plane of their orbits need not lie near the ecliptic. Long-period comets such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/1999_F1" title="C/1999 F1">C/1999 F1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2017_T2_(PANSTARRS)" title="C/2017 T2 (PANSTARRS)">C/2017 T2 (PANSTARRS)</a> can have aphelion distances of nearly 70,000 AU (0.34 pc; 1.1 ly) with orbital periods estimated around 6 million years.
</p><p>Single-apparition or non-periodic comets are similar to long-period comets because they also have <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trajectory" title="Parabolic trajectory">parabolic</a> or slightly <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">hyperbolic</a> trajectories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBP_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-SBP-96">[96]</a></sup> when near perihelion in the inner Solar System. However, gravitational perturbations from giant planets cause their orbits to change. Single-apparition comets have a hyperbolic or parabolic <a href="/wiki/Osculating_orbit" title="Osculating orbit">osculating orbit</a> which allows them to permanently exit the Solar System after a single pass of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The Sun's <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a> has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unstable" title="Unstable">unstable</a> maximum boundary of 230,000 AU (1.1 pc; 3.6 ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chebotarev1964_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chebotarev1964-99">[99]</a></sup> Only a few hundred comets have been seen to reach a hyperbolic orbit (e &gt; 1) when near perihelion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e1_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-e1-100">[100]</a></sup> that using a heliocentric unperturbed <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem" title="Two-body problem">two-body</a> <a href="/wiki/Curve_fitting" title="Curve fitting">best-fit</a> suggests they may escape the Solar System.
</p><p>As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only two objects have been discovered with an <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a> significantly greater than one: <a href="/wiki/%CA%BBOumuamua" title="ʻOumuamua">1I/ʻOumuamua</a> and <a href="/wiki/2I/Borisov" title="2I/Borisov">2I/Borisov</a>, indicating an origin outside the Solar System. While ʻOumuamua, with an eccentricity of about 1.2, showed no optical signs of cometary activity during its passage through the inner Solar System in October 2017, changes to its trajectory—which suggests <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a>—indicate that it is probably a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oumuamua_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oumuamua-101">[101]</a></sup> On the other hand, 2I/Borisov, with an estimated eccentricity of about 3.36, has been observed to have the coma feature of comets, and is considered the first detected <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_object" title="Interstellar object">interstellar comet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-103">[103]</a></sup> Comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/1980_E1" title="C/1980 E1">C/1980 E1</a> had an orbital period of roughly 7.1 million years before the 1982 perihelion passage, but a 1980 encounter with Jupiter accelerated the comet giving it the largest eccentricity (1.057) of any known solar comet with a reasonable observation arc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C/1980E1-jpl_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-C/1980E1-jpl-104">[104]</a></sup> Comets not expected to return to the inner Solar System include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/1980_E1" title="C/1980 E1">C/1980 E1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2000_U5" title="C/2000 U5">C/2000 U5</a>, <a href="/wiki/C/2001_Q4_(NEAT)" title="C/2001 Q4 (NEAT)">C/2001 Q4 (NEAT)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2009_R1" title="C/2009 R1">C/2009 R1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/1956_R1" title="C/1956 R1">C/1956 R1</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2007_F1" title="C/2007 F1">C/2007 F1</a> (LONEOS).
</p><p>Some authorities use the term "periodic comet" to refer to any comet with a periodic orbit (that is, all short-period comets plus all long-period comets),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> whereas others use it to mean exclusively short-period comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBP_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-SBP-96">[96]</a></sup> Similarly, although the literal meaning of "non-periodic comet" is the same as "single-apparition comet", some use it to mean all comets that are not "periodic" in the second sense (that is, to also include all comets with a period greater than 200 years).
</p><p>Early observations have revealed a few genuinely hyperbolic (i.e. non-periodic) trajectories, but no more than could be accounted for by perturbations from Jupiter. Comets from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a> are moving with velocities of the same order as the relative velocities of stars near the Sun (a few tens of km per second). When such objects enter the Solar System, they have a positive <a href="/wiki/Specific_orbital_energy" title="Specific orbital energy">specific orbital energy</a> resulting in a positive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_excess_velocity" title="Hyperbolic excess velocity">velocity at infinity</a> (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{\infty }\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{\infty }\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle v_{\infty }\!}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4439fe0f5c56e071edb64af0bd2c916211475fe4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:3.003ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>) and have notably hyperbolic trajectories. A rough calculation shows that there might be four hyperbolic comets per century within Jupiter's orbit, give or take one and perhaps two orders of <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">magnitude</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
