<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oort_cloud_and_Hills_cloud">Oort cloud and Hills cloud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Oort cloud and Hills cloud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/220px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/330px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg/440px-Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_belt_-_Oort_cloud-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> thought to surround the Solar System</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a></div>
<p>The Oort cloud is thought to occupy a vast space starting from between 2,000 and 5,000 AU (0.03 and 0.08 ly)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-108">[108]</a></sup> to as far as 50,000 AU (0.79 ly)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2006_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2006-84">[84]</a></sup> from the Sun. This cloud encases the celestial bodies that start at the middle of our solar system—the sun, all the way to outer limits of the Kuiper Belt. The Oort cloud consists of viable materials necessary for the creation of celestial bodies. The planets we have today, exist only because of the planetesimals (chunks of leftover space that assisted in the creation of planets) that were condensed and formed by the gravity of the sun. The eccentric made from these trapped planetesimals is why the Oort Cloud even exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Some estimates place the outer edge at between 100,000 and 200,000 AU (1.58 and 3.16 ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-book-108">[108]</a></sup> The region can be subdivided into a spherical outer Oort cloud of 20,000–50,000 AU (0.32–0.79 ly), and a doughnut-shaped inner cloud, the Hills cloud, of 2,000–20,000 AU (0.03–0.32 ly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The outer cloud is only weakly bound to the Sun and supplies the long-period (and possibly Halley-type) comets that fall to inside the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2006_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2006-84">[84]</a></sup> The inner Oort cloud is also known as the Hills cloud, named after J. G. Hills, who proposed its existence in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hills1981_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-hills1981-111">[111]</a></sup> Models predict that the inner cloud should have tens or hundreds of times as many cometary nuclei as the outer halo;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hills1981_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-hills1981-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levison2001_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-levison2001-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donahue1991_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donahue1991-113">[113]</a></sup> it is seen as a possible source of new comets that resupply the relatively tenuous outer cloud as the latter's numbers are gradually depleted. The Hills cloud explains the continued existence of the Oort cloud after billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julio1997_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Julio1997-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exocomets">Exocomets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Exocomets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exocomet" title="Exocomet">Exocomet</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Exocomet" title="Exocomet">Exocomets</a> beyond the Solar System have also been detected and may be common in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berk_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-berk-115">[115]</a></sup> The first exocomet system detected was around <a href="/wiki/Beta_Pictoris" title="Beta Pictoris">Beta Pictoris</a>, a very young <a href="/wiki/A-type_main-sequence_star" title="A-type main-sequence star">A-type main-sequence star</a>, in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130107_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130107-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beust1990_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beust1990-117">[117]</a></sup> A total of 11 such exocomet systems have been identified as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, using the <a href="/wiki/Absorption_spectroscopy" title="Absorption spectroscopy">absorption spectrum</a> caused by the large clouds of gas emitted by comets when passing close to their star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berk_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-berk-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130107_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130107-116">[116]</a></sup> For ten years the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_space_telescope" title="Kepler space telescope">Kepler space telescope</a> was responsible for searching for planets and other forms outside of the solar system. The first transiting exocomets were found in February 2018 by a group consisting of professional astronomers and <a href="/wiki/Citizen_science" title="Citizen science">citizen scientists</a> in light curves recorded by the Kepler Space Telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> After Kepler Space Telescope retired in October 2018, a new telescope called TESS Telescope has taken over Kepler's mission. Since the launch of TESS, astronomers have discovered the transits of comets around the star Beta Pictoris using a light curve from TESS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Since TESS has taken over, astronomers have since been able to better distinguish exocomets with the spectroscopic method. New planets are detected by the white light curve method which is viewed as a symmetrical dip in the charts readings when a planet overshadows its parent star. However, after further evaluation of these light curves, it has been discovered that the asymmetrical patterns of the dips presented are caused by the tail of a comet or of hundreds of comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_comets">Effects of comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects of comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1178" data-file-width="1419" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/220px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/330px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/440px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of <a href="/wiki/Perseids" title="Perseids">Perseids meteors</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_to_meteor_showers">Connection to meteor showers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Connection to meteor showers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a comet is heated during close passes to the Sun, <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> of its icy components also releases solid debris too large to be swept away by <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> and the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> If Earth's orbit sends it through that trail of debris, which is composed mostly of fine grains of rocky material, there is likely to be a <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">meteor shower</a> as Earth passes through. Denser trails of debris produce quick but intense meteor showers and less dense trails create longer but less intense showers. Typically, the density of the debris trail is related to how long ago the parent comet released the material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam19990920_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam19990920-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo20190207_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo20190207-125">[125]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Perseids" title="Perseids">Perseid meteor shower</a>, for example, occurs every year between 9 and 13 August, when Earth passes through the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Swift%E2%80%93Tuttle" title="Comet Swift–Tuttle">Comet Swift–Tuttle</a>. Halley's Comet is the source of the <a href="/wiki/Orionids" title="Orionids">Orionid shower</a> in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-showers_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-showers-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comets_and_impact_on_life">Comets and impact on life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Comets and impact on life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many comets and asteroids collided with Earth in its early stages. Many scientists think that comets bombarding the young Earth about 4 billion years ago brought the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">vast quantities of water</a> that now fill Earth's oceans, or at least a significant portion of it. Others have cast doubt on this idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The detection of organic molecules, including <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150210_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150210-18">[18]</a></sup> in significant quantities in comets has led to speculation that comets or <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> may have brought the precursors of life—or even life itself—to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In 2013 it was suggested that impacts between rocky and icy surfaces, such as comets, had the potential to create the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> that make up <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> through <a href="/wiki/Shock_synthesis" title="Shock synthesis">shock synthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The speed at which the comets entered the atmosphere, combined with the magnitude of energy created after initial contact, allowed smaller molecules to condense into the larger macro-molecules that served as the foundation for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In 2015, scientists found significant amounts of molecular oxygen in the outgassings of comet 67P, suggesting that the molecule may occur more often than had been thought, and thus less an indicator of life as has been supposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is suspected that comet impacts have, over long timescales, also delivered significant quantities of water to Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, some of which may have survived as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_ice" title="Lunar ice">lunar ice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Comet and <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroid</a> impacts are also thought to be responsible for the existence of <a href="/wiki/Tektite" title="Tektite">tektites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australite" title="Australite">australites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fear_of_comets">Fear of comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fear of comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fear of comets as <a href="/wiki/Act_of_God" title="Act of God">acts of God</a> and signs of impending doom was highest in Europe from AD 1200 to 1650.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196710_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley196710-135">[135]</a></sup> The year after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1618" title="Great Comet of 1618">Great Comet of 1618</a>, for example, <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Arthusius" title="Gotthard Arthusius">Gotthard Arthusius</a> published a pamphlet stating that it was a sign that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Judgment" title="Day of Judgment">Day of Judgment</a> was near.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> He listed ten pages of comet-related disasters, including "earthquakes, floods, changes in river courses, hail storms, hot and dry weather, poor harvests, epidemics, war and treason and high prices".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196710_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-ley196710-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1700 most scholars concluded that such events occurred whether a comet was seen or not. Using <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a>'s records of comet sightings, however, <a href="/wiki/William_Whiston" title="William Whiston">William Whiston</a> in 1711 wrote that the <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1680" title="Great Comet of 1680">Great Comet of 1680</a> had a periodicity of 574 years and was responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_flood_narrative" title="Genesis flood narrative">worldwide flood in the Book of Genesis</a>, by pouring water on Earth. His announcement revived for another century fear of comets, now as direct threats to the world instead of signs of disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196710_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-ley196710-135">[135]</a></sup> Spectroscopic analysis in 1910 found the toxic gas <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a> in the tail of Halley's Comet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> causing panicked buying of gas masks and quack "anti-comet pills" and "anti-comet umbrellas" by the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fate_of_comets">Fate of comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fate of comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Departure_.28ejection.29_from_Solar_System"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure_(ejection)_from_Solar_System">Departure (ejection) from Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Departure (ejection) from Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If a comet is traveling fast enough, it may leave the Solar System. Such comets follow the open path of a hyperbola, and as such, they are called hyperbolic comets. Solar comets are only known to be ejected by <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">interacting</a> with another object in the Solar System, such as Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> An example of this is Comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/1980_E1" title="C/1980 E1">C/1980 E1</a>, which was shifted from an orbit of 7.1 million years around the Sun, to a hyperbolic trajectory, after a 1980 close pass by the planet Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barycenter_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-barycenter-140">[140]</a></sup> Interstellar comets such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%27Oumuamua" title="'Oumuamua">1I/ʻOumuamua</a> and <a href="/wiki/2I/Borisov" title="2I/Borisov">2I/Borisov</a> never orbited the Sun and therefore do not require a 3rd-body interaction to be ejected from the Solar System.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volatiles_exhausted">Volatiles exhausted</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Volatiles exhausted">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extinct_comet" title="Extinct comet">Extinct comet</a></div>
<p>Jupiter-family comets and long-period comets appear to follow very different fading laws. The JFCs are active over a lifetime of about 10,000 years or ~1,000 orbits whereas long-period comets fade much faster. Only 10% of the long-period comets survive more than 50 passages to small perihelion and only 1% of them survive more than 2,000 passages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dormant_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-dormant-32">[32]</a></sup> Eventually most of the volatile material contained in a comet nucleus evaporates, and the comet becomes a small, dark, inert lump of rock or rubble that can resemble an asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Some asteroids in elliptical orbits are now identified as extinct comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Roughly six percent of the near-Earth asteroids are thought to be extinct comet nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dormant_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-dormant-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakup_and_collisions">Breakup and collisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Breakup and collisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The nucleus of some comets may be fragile, a conclusion supported by the observation of comets splitting apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> A significant cometary disruption was that of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9</a>, which was discovered in 1993. A close encounter in July 1992 had broken it into pieces, and over a period of six days in July 1994, these pieces fell into Jupiter's atmosphere—the first time astronomers had observed a collision between two objects in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Other splitting comets include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3D/Biela" title="3D/Biela">3D/Biela</a> in 1846 and <a href="/wiki/73P/Schwassmann%E2%80%93Wachmann" title="73P/Schwassmann–Wachmann">73P/Schwassmann–Wachmann</a> from 1995 to 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer2006_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer2006-149">[149]</a></sup> Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a> reported that a comet split apart as far back as the winter of 372–373 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-great_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-great-150">[150]</a></sup> Comets are suspected of splitting due to thermal stress, internal gas pressure, or impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-split_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-split-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comets <a href="/wiki/42P/Neujmin" title="42P/Neujmin">42P/Neujmin</a> and <a href="/wiki/53P/Van_Biesbroeck" title="53P/Van Biesbroeck">53P/Van Biesbroeck</a> appear to be fragments of a parent comet. Numerical integrations have shown that both comets had a rather close approach to Jupiter in January 1850, and that, before 1850, the two orbits were nearly identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPS35_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-DPS35-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some comets have been observed to break up during their perihelion passage, including great comets <a href="/wiki/Comet_West" title="Comet West">West</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet_Ikeya%E2%80%93Seki" title="Comet Ikeya–Seki">Ikeya–Seki</a>. Biela's Comet was one significant example when it broke into two pieces during its passage through the perihelion in 1846. These two comets were seen separately in 1852, but never again afterward. Instead, spectacular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor_showers" title="Meteor showers">meteor showers</a> were seen in 1872 and 1885 when the comet should have been visible. A minor meteor shower, the <a href="/wiki/Andromedids" title="Andromedids">Andromedids</a>, occurs annually in November, and it is caused when Earth crosses the orbit of Biela's Comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some comets meet a more spectacular end – either falling into the Sun<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> or smashing into a planet or other body. Collisions between comets and planets or moons were common in the early Solar System: some of the many craters on the Moon, for example, may have been caused by comets. A recent collision of a comet with a planet occurred in July 1994 when Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9 broke up into pieces and collided with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:792px;max-width:792px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:226px;max-width:226px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:176px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/224px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/336px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/448px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg 2x" width="224"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Brown spots
