<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naming_of_comets" title="Naming of comets">Naming of comets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Halley%27s_Comet,_1910.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG/220px-Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG/330px-Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG/440px-Halley%27s_Comet%2C_1910.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Halley%27s_Comet,_1910.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a> in 1910</div></div></div>
<p>The names given to comets have followed several different conventions over the past two centuries. Prior to the early 20th century, most comets were simply referred to by the year when they appeared, sometimes with additional adjectives for particularly bright comets; thus, the "Great Comet of 1680", the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1882" title="Great Comet of 1882">Great Comet of 1882</a>", and the "<a href="/wiki/Great_January_Comet_of_1910" title="Great January Comet of 1910">Great January Comet of 1910</a>".
</p><p>After Edmond Halley demonstrated that the comets of 1531, 1607, and 1682 were the same body and successfully predicted its return in 1759 by calculating its orbit, that comet became known as Halley's Comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Similarly, the second and third known periodic comets, Encke's Comet<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KronkEncke_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-KronkEncke-158">[158]</a></sup> and Biela's Comet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KronkBiela_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-KronkBiela-159">[159]</a></sup> were named after the astronomers who calculated their orbits rather than their original discoverers. Later, periodic comets were usually named after their discoverers, but comets that had appeared only once continued to be referred to by the year of their appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvcom_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvcom-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 20th century, the convention of naming comets after their discoverers became common, and this remains so today. A comet can be named after its discoverers or an instrument or program that helped to find it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvcom_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-harvcom-160">[160]</a></sup> For example, in 2019, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Gennadiy_Borisov" title="Gennadiy Borisov">Gennadiy Borisov</a> observed a comet that appeared to have originated outside of the solar system; the comet was named <a href="/wiki/2I/Borisov" title="2I/Borisov">2I/Borisov</a> after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen2019_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen2019-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: History of study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Observational_history_of_comets" title="Observational history of comets">Observational history of comets</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_observations_and_thought">Early observations and thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Early observations and thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:237px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2583" data-file-width="2482" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg/235px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg/353px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg/470px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg 2x" width="235"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene32_Halley_comet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a> appeared in 1066, prior to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">Battle of Hastings</a>, and is depicted in the <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="398" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png/220px-Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png/330px-Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tychonis_Brahe_De_mundi_aetherei_recentioribus_phaenomenis_liber_secundus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from a treatise by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> depicting his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">geocentric</a> view of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1577" title="Great Comet of 1577">Great Comet of 1577</a></div></div></div>
<p>From ancient sources, such as Chinese <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a>, it is known that comets have been noticed by humans for millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Until the sixteenth century, comets were usually considered bad <a href="/wiki/Omen" title="Omen">omens</a> of deaths of kings or noble men, or coming catastrophes, or even interpreted as attacks by heavenly beings against terrestrial inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–322 BC) was the first known scientist to utilize various theories and observational facts to employ a consistent, structured cosmological theory of comets. He believed that comets were atmospheric phenomena, due to the fact that they could appear outside of the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a> and vary in brightness over the course of a few days. Aristotle's cometary theory arose from his observations and cosmological theory that everything in the cosmos is arranged in a distinct configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Part of this configuration was a clear separation between the celestial and terrestrial, believing comets to be strictly associated with the latter. According to Aristotle, comets must be within the sphere of the moon and clearly separated from the heavens. Also in the 4th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Myndus" title="Apollonius of Myndus">Apollonius of Myndus</a> supported the idea that comets moved like the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199748_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199748-166">[166]</a></sup> Aristotelian theory on comets continued to be widely accepted throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, despite several discoveries from various individuals challenging aspects of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1st century AD, <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a> questioned Aristotle's logic concerning comets. Because of their regular movement and imperviousness to wind, they cannot be atmospheric,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726-168">[168]</a></sup> and are more permanent than suggested by their brief flashes across the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[a]</a></sup> He pointed out that only the tails are transparent and thus cloudlike, and argued that there is no reason to confine their orbits to the zodiac.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726-168">[168]</a></sup> In criticizing Apollonius of Myndus, Seneca argues, "A comet cuts through the upper regions of the universe and then finally becomes visible when it reaches the lowest point of its orbit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726–27_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726–27-170">[169]</a></sup> While Seneca did not author a substantial theory of his own,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> his arguments would spark much debate among Aristotle's critics in the 16th and 17th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in the 1st century, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> believed that comets were connected with political unrest and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> Pliny observed comets as "human like", often describing their tails with "long hair" or "long beard".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup> His system for classifying comets according to their color and shape was used for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-176">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">India</a>, by the 6th century astronomers believed that comets were celestial bodies that re-appeared periodically. This was the view expressed in the 6th century by the astronomers <a href="/wiki/Var%C4%81hamihira" title="Varāhamihira">Varāhamihira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhadrabahu_III" title="Bhadrabahu III">Bhadrabahu</a>, and the 10th-century astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADotpala" title="Bhaṭṭotpala">Bhaṭṭotpala</a> listed the names and estimated periods of certain comets, but it is not known how these figures were calculated or how accurate they were.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> In 1301, the Italian painter <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> was the first person to accurately and anatomically portray a comet. In his work <i><a href="/wiki/Adoration_of_the_Magi" title="Adoration of the Magi">Adoration of the Magi</a>,</i> Giotto's depiction of Halley's Comet in the place of the <a href="/wiki/Star_of_Bethlehem" title="Star of Bethlehem">Star of Bethlehem</a> would go unmatched in accuracy until the 19th century and be bested only with the invention of photography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-178">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Astrological interpretations of comets proceeded to take precedence clear into the 15th century, despite the presence of modern scientific astronomy beginning to take root. Comets continued to forewarn of disaster, as seen in the <i><a href="/wiki/Luzerner_Schilling" title="Luzerner Schilling">Luzerner Schilling</a></i> chronicles and in the warnings of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Callixtus_III" title="Pope Callixtus III">Pope Callixtus III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-178">[176]</a></sup> In 1578, German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> bishop Andreas Celichius defined comets as "the thick smoke of human sins ... kindled by the hot and fiery anger of the <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">Supreme Heavenly Judge</a>". The next year, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Dudith" title="Andreas Dudith">Andreas Dudith</a> stated that "If comets were caused by the sins of mortals, they would never be absent from the sky."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199732–33_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199732–33-179">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_approach">Scientific approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Scientific approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Crude attempts at a <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> measurement of Halley's Comet were made in 1456, but were erroneous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199736_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199736-180">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Regiomontanus" title="Regiomontanus">Regiomontanus</a> was the first to attempt to calculate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_parallax" title="Diurnal parallax">diurnal parallax</a> by observing the great comet of 1472. His predictions were not very accurate, but they were conducted in the hopes of estimating the distance of a comet from the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-176">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Maestlin" title="Michael Maestlin">Michael Maestlin</a> demonstrated that comets must exist outside of Earth's atmosphere by measuring the parallax of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1577" title="Great Comet of 1577">Great Comet of 1577</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> Within the precision of the measurements, this implied the comet must be at least four times more distant than from Earth to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESO_part_I_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESO_part_I-182">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> Based on observations in 1664, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Alfonso_Borelli" title="Giovanni Alfonso Borelli">Giovanni Borelli</a> recorded the longitudes and latitudes of comets that he observed, and suggested that cometary orbits may be parabolic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, one of the most renowned astronomers to date, even attempted writings on comets in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i>. He rejected Brahe's theories on the parallax of comets and claimed that they may be a mere optical illusion. Intrigued as early scientists were about the nature of comets, Galileo could not help but throw about his own theories despite little personal observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-176">[174]</a></sup> Maestlin's student <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> responded to these unjust criticisms in his work <i>Hyperaspistes.</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jakob_Bernoulli" title="Jakob Bernoulli">Jakob Bernoulli</a> published another attempt to explain comets (Conamen Novi Systematis Cometarum) in 1682.
</p><p>Also occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> was the study of comets and their astrological significance in medical disciplines. Many healers of this time considered medicine and astronomy to be inter-disciplinary and employed their knowledge of comets and other astrological signs for diagnosing and treating patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i> of 1687, proved that an object moving under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> by an inverse square law must trace out an orbit shaped like one of the <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a>, and he demonstrated how to fit a comet's path through the sky to a parabolic orbit, using the comet of 1680 as an example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup>
He describes comets as compact and durable solid bodies moving in oblique orbit and their tails as thin streams of vapor emitted by their nuclei, ignited or heated by the Sun. He suspected that comets were the origin of the life-supporting component of air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup> He also pointed out that comets usually appear near the Sun, and therefore most likely orbit it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726-168">[168]</a></sup> On their luminosity, he stated, "The comets shine by the Sun's light, which they reflect," with their tails illuminated by "the Sun's light reflected by a smoke arising from [the coma]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199726-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newton_Comet1680.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="1506" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Newton_Comet1680.jpg/290px-Newton_Comet1680.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Newton_Comet1680.jpg/435px-Newton_Comet1680.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Newton_Comet1680.jpg/580px-Newton_Comet1680.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newton_Comet1680.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The orbit of the comet of 1680, fitted to a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, as shown in <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In 1705, Edmond Halley (1656–1742) applied Newton's method to 23 cometary apparitions that had occurred between 1337 and 1698. He noted that three of these, the comets of 1531, 1607, and 1682, had very similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_element" title="Orbital element">orbital elements</a>, and he was further able to account for the slight differences in their orbits in terms of gravitational perturbation caused by Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. Confident that these three apparitions had been three appearances of the same comet, he predicted that it would appear again in 1758–9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halley_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-188">[186]</a></sup> Halley's predicted return date was later refined by a team of three French mathematicians: <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Clairaut" title="Alexis Clairaut">Alexis Clairaut</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lalande" title="Joseph Lalande">Joseph Lalande</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nicole-Reine_Lepaute" title="Nicole-Reine Lepaute">Nicole-Reine Lepaute</a>, who predicted the date of the comet's 1759 perihelion to within one month's accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199793_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199793-190">[188]</a></sup> When the comet returned as predicted, it became known as Halley's Comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="; padding: 8px 12px;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>From his huge vapouring train perhaps to shake<br/>Reviving moisture on the numerous orbs,<br/>Thro' which his long ellipsis winds; perhaps<br/>To lend new fuel to declining suns,<br/>To light up worlds, and feed th' ethereal fire.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Thomson_(poet)" title="James Thomson (poet)">James Thomson</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Seasons_(Thomson_poem)" title="The Seasons (Thomson poem)">The Seasons</a></i> (1730; 1748)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>As early as the 18th century, some scientists had made correct hypotheses as to comets' physical composition. In 1755, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> hypothesized in his <i><a href="/wiki/Universal_Natural_History_and_Theory_of_the_Heavens" title="Universal Natural History and Theory of the Heavens">Universal Natural History</a></i> that comets were condensed from "primitive matter" beyond the known planets, which is "feebly moved" by gravity, then orbit at arbitrary inclinations, and are partially vaporized by the Sun's heat as they near perihelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199784–87_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199784–87-193">[191]</a></sup> In 1836, the German mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel">Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel</a>, after observing streams of vapor during the appearance of Halley's Comet in 1835, proposed that the <a href="/wiki/Jet_force" title="Jet force">jet forces</a> of evaporating material could be great enough to significantly alter a comet's orbit, and he argued that the non-gravitational movements of Encke's Comet resulted from this phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 19th century, the Astronomical Observatory of Padova was an epicenter in the observational study of comets. Led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giovanni_Santini" title="Giovanni Santini">Giovanni Santini</a> (1787–1877) and followed by Giuseppe Lorenzoni (1843–1914), this observatory was devoted to classical astronomy, mainly to the new comets and planets orbit calculation, with the goal of compiling a catalog of almost ten thousand stars. Situated in the Northern portion of Italy, observations from this observatory were key in establishing important geodetic, geographic, and astronomical calculations, such as the difference of longitude between Milan and Padua as well as Padua to Fiume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> In addition to these geographic observations, correspondence within the observatory, particularly between Santini and another astronomer Giuseppe Toaldo, about the importance of comet and planetary orbital observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Lawrence_Whipple" title="Fred Lawrence Whipple">Fred Lawrence Whipple</a> proposed that rather than being rocky objects containing some ice, comets were icy objects containing some dust and rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whipple_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whipple-197">[195]</a></sup> This "dirty snowball" model soon became accepted and appeared to be supported by the observations of an armada of <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giotto_mission" title="Giotto mission">Giotto</a></i> probe and the Soviet Union's <i><a href="/wiki/Vega_1" title="Vega 1">Vega 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vega_2" title="Vega 2">Vega 2</a></i>) that flew through the coma of Halley's Comet in 1986, photographed the nucleus, and observed jets of evaporating material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 January 2014, <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA scientists</a> reported the detection, for the first definitive time, of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, the largest object in the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KüppersO’Rourke2014_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-KüppersO’Rourke2014-199">[197]</a></sup> The detection was made by using the <a href="/wiki/Far-infrared_astronomy" title="Far-infrared astronomy">far-infrared abilities</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140122_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140122-200">[198]</a></sup> The finding is unexpected because comets, not asteroids, are typically considered to "sprout jets and plumes". According to one of the scientists, "The lines are becoming more and more blurred between comets and asteroids."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140122_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140122-200">[198]</a></sup> On 11 August 2014, astronomers released studies, using the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">Atacama Large Millimeter/Submillimeter Array (ALMA)</a> for the first time, that detailed the distribution of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">HCN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_isocyanide" title="Hydrogen isocyanide">HNC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>CO</span></a>, and dust inside the <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">comae</a> of comets <a href="/wiki/C/2012_F6_(Lemmon)" title="C/2012 F6 (Lemmon)">C/2012 F6 (Lemmon)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet_ISON" title="Comet ISON">C/2012 S1 (ISON)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140811_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140811-201">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20140811_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20140811-202">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_missions">Spacecraft missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Spacecraft missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_comets_visited_by_spacecraft" title="List of comets visited by spacecraft">List of comets visited by spacecraft</a></div>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Halley_Armada" title="Halley Armada">Halley Armada</a> describes the collection of spacecraft missions that visited and/or made observations of Halley's Comet 1980s perihelion. The space shuttle <i>Challenger</i> was intended to do a study of Halley's Comet in 1986, but exploded shortly after being launched.</li>
<li><b>Deep Impact</b>. Debate continues about how much ice is in a comet. In 2001, the <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_1" title="Deep Space 1">Deep Space 1</a></i> spacecraft obtained high-resolution images of the surface of <a href="/wiki/19P/Borrelly" title="19P/Borrelly">Comet Borrelly</a>. It was found that the surface of comet Borrelly is hot and dry, with a temperature of between 26 to 71 °C (79 to 160 °F), and extremely dark, suggesting that the ice has been removed by solar heating and maturation, or is hidden by the soot-like material that covers Borrelly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> In July 2005, the <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i> probe blasted a crater on Comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> to study its interior. The mission yielded results suggesting that the majority of a comet's water ice is below the surface and that these reservoirs feed the jets of vaporized water that form the coma of Tempel 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup> Renamed <a href="/wiki/EPOXI" title="EPOXI">EPOXI</a>, it made a flyby of <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">Comet Hartley 2</a> on 4 November 2010.</li>
<li><b>Ulysses</b>. In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft)" title="Ulysses (spacecraft)">Ulysses probe</a> unexpectedly passed through the tail of the comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2006_P1_(McNaught)" title="C/2006 P1 (McNaught)">C/2006 P1</a> (McNaught) which was discovered in 2006. Ulysses was launched in 1990 and the intended mission was for Ulysses to orbit around the sun for further study at all latitudes.</li>
<li><b>Stardust</b>. Data from the <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)"><i>Stardust</i> mission</a> show that materials retrieved from the tail of Wild 2 were crystalline and could only have been "born in fire", at extremely high temperatures of over 1,000 °C (1,830 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> Although comets formed in the outer Solar System, radial mixing of material during the early formation of the Solar System is thought to have redistributed material throughout the proto-planetary disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> As a result, comets also contain crystalline grains that formed in the early, hot inner Solar System. This is seen in comet spectra as well as in sample return missions. More recent still, the materials retrieved demonstrate that the "comet dust resembles asteroid materials".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup> These new results have forced scientists to rethink the nature of comets and their distinction from asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Rosetta</b>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">Rosetta</a></i> probe orbited <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov–Gerasimenko">Comet Churyumov–Gerasimenko</a>. On 12 November 2014, its lander <i>Philae</i> successfully landed on the comet's surface, the first time a spacecraft has ever landed on such an object in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_comets">Great comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Great comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_comet" title="Great comet">Great comet</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1577" title="Great Comet of 1577">Great Comet of 1577</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Von_einem_Schrecklichen_vnd_Wunderbarlichen_Cometen_so_sich_den_Dienstag_nach_Martini_dieses_lauffenden_M._D._Lxxvij._Jahrs_am_Himmel_erzeiget_hat_(grayscale).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3546" data-file-width="4814" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Von_einem_Schrecklichen_vnd_Wunderbarlichen_Cometen_so_sich_den_Dienstag_nach_Martini_dieses_lauffenden_M._D._Lxxvij._Jahrs_am_Himmel_erzeiget_hat_%28grayscale%29.png/300px-thumbnail.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Von_einem_Schrecklichen_vnd_Wunderbarlichen_Cometen_so_sich_den_Dienstag_nach_Martini_dieses_lauffenden_M._D._Lxxvij._Jahrs_am_Himmel_erzeiget_hat_%28grayscale%29.png/450px-thumbnail.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Von_einem_Schrecklichen_vnd_Wunderbarlichen_Cometen_so_sich_den_Dienstag_nach_Martini_dieses_lauffenden_M._D._Lxxvij._Jahrs_am_Himmel_erzeiget_hat_%28grayscale%29.png/600px-thumbnail.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Von_einem_Schrecklichen_vnd_Wunderbarlichen_Cometen_so_sich_den_Dienstag_nach_Martini_dieses_lauffenden_M._D._Lxxvij._Jahrs_am_Himmel_erzeiget_hat_(grayscale).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1577" title="Great Comet of 1577">Great Comet of 1577</a></div></div></div>
<p>Approximately once a decade, a comet becomes bright enough to be noticed by a casual observer, leading such comets to be designated as great comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-great_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-great-150">[150]</a></sup> Predicting whether a comet will become a great comet is notoriously difficult, as many factors may cause a comet's brightness to depart drastically from predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup> Broadly speaking, if a comet has a large and active nucleus, will pass close to the Sun, and is not obscured by the Sun as seen from Earth when at its brightest, it has a chance of becoming a great comet. However, <a href="/wiki/Comet_Kohoutek" title="Comet Kohoutek">Comet Kohoutek</a> in 1973 fulfilled all the criteria and was expected to become spectacular but failed to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Comet_West" title="Comet West">Comet West</a>, which appeared three years later, had much lower expectations but became an extremely impressive comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Comet of 1577 is a well-known example of a great comet. It passed near Earth as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_near-parabolic_comets" title="List of near-parabolic comets">non-periodic comet</a> and was seen by many, including well-known astronomers Tycho Brahe and <a href="/wiki/Taqi_ad-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf" title="Taqi ad-Din Muhammad ibn Ma'ruf">Taqi ad-Din</a>. Observations of this comet led to several significant findings regarding cometary science, especially for Brahe.
</p><p>The late 20th century saw a lengthy gap without the appearance of any great comets, followed by the arrival of two in quick succession—<a href="/wiki/Comet_Hyakutake" title="Comet Hyakutake">Comet Hyakutake</a> in 1996, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hale%E2%80%93Bopp" title="Hale–Bopp">Hale–Bopp</a>, which reached maximum brightness in 1997 having been discovered two years earlier. The first great comet of the 21st century was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C/2006_P1" title="C/2006 P1">C/2006 P1</a> (McNaught), which became visible to naked eye observers in January 2007. It was the brightest in over 40 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sungrazing_comets">Sungrazing comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Sungrazing comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sungrazing_comet" title="Sungrazing comet">Sungrazing comet</a></div>
<p>A sungrazing comet is a comet that passes extremely close to the Sun at perihelion, generally within a few million kilometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> Although small sungrazers can be completely evaporated during such a close approach to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, larger sungrazers can survive many perihelion passages. However, the strong <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> they experience often lead to their fragmentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 90% of the sungrazers observed with <a href="/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory" title="Solar and Heliospheric Observatory">SOHO</a> are members of the <a href="/wiki/Kreutz_sungrazer" title="Kreutz sungrazer">Kreutz group</a>, which all originate from one giant comet that broke up into many smaller comets during its first passage through the inner Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-217">[215]</a></sup> The remainder contains some sporadic sungrazers, but four other related groups of comets have been identified among them: the Kracht, Kracht 2a, Marsden, and Meyer groups. The Marsden and Kracht groups both appear to be related to <a href="/wiki/96P/Machholz" title="96P/Machholz">Comet 96P/Machholz</a>, which is also the parent of two <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">meteor streams</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quadrantids" title="Quadrantids">Quadrantids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arietids" title="Arietids">Arietids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohtsuka_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ohtsuka-218">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unusual_comets">Unusual comets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Unusual comets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="#Fate_of_comets">§ Fate of comets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swastika#Comet" title="Swastika">Swastika § Comet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="635" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/300px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/450px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg/600px-Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euler_diagram_of_solar_system_bodies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> showing the types of bodies in the Solar System</div></div></div>
<p>Of the thousands of known comets, some exhibit unusual properties. Comet Encke (2P/Encke) orbits from outside the asteroid belt to just inside the orbit of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> whereas the Comet <a href="/wiki/29P/Schwassmann%E2%80%93Wachmann" title="29P/Schwassmann–Wachmann">29P/Schwassmann–Wachmann</a> currently travels in a nearly circular orbit entirely between the orbits of Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">2060 Chiron</a>, whose unstable orbit is between Saturn and <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, was originally classified as an asteroid until a faint coma was noticed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup> Similarly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/137P/Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy" title="137P/Shoemaker–Levy">Comet Shoemaker–Levy 2</a> was originally designated asteroid <span class="nowrap">1990 UL<sub>3</sub></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest">Largest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Largest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The largest known periodic comet is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/95P/Chiron" title="95P/Chiron">95P/Chiron</a> at 200 km in diameter that comes to perihelion every 50 years just inside of Saturn's orbit at 8 AU. The largest known Oort cloud comet is suspected of being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Bernardinelli-Bernstein" title="Comet Bernardinelli-Bernstein">Comet Bernardinelli-Bernstein</a> at ≈150 km that will not come to perihelion until January 2031 just outside of Saturn's orbit at 11 AU. The <a href="/wiki/Comet_of_1729" title="Comet of 1729">Comet of 1729</a> is estimated to have been ≈100 km in diameter and came to perihelion inside of Jupiter's orbit at 4 AU.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centaurs">Centaurs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Centaurs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">Centaur (minor planet)</a></div>
<p>Centaurs typically behave with characteristics of both asteroids and comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horner2004a_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horner2004a-222">[220]</a></sup> Centaurs can be classified as comets such as <a href="/wiki/60558_Echeclus" title="60558 Echeclus">60558 Echeclus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/166P/NEAT" title="166P/NEAT">166P/NEAT</a>. 166P/NEAT was discovered while it exhibited a coma, and so is classified as a comet despite its orbit, and <a href="/wiki/60558_Echeclus" title="60558 Echeclus">60558 Echeclus</a> was discovered without a coma but later became active,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choietal2006_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choietal2006-223">[221]</a></sup> and was then classified as both a comet and an asteroid (174P/Echeclus). One plan for <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i> involved sending it to a centaur, but NASA decided to destroy it instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usra_0903_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-usra_0903-224">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A comet may be discovered photographically using a wide-field <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> or visually with <a href="/wiki/Binoculars" title="Binoculars">binoculars</a>. However, even without access to optical equipment, it is still possible for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomer" title="Amateur astronomer">amateur astronomer</a> to discover a sungrazing comet online by downloading images accumulated by some satellite observatories such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory" title="Solar and Heliospheric Observatory">SOHO</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soho2_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-soho2-225">[223]</a></sup> SOHO's 2000th comet was discovered by Polish amateur astronomer Michał Kusiak on 26 December 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> and both discoverers of Hale–Bopp used amateur equipment (although Hale was not an amateur).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lost">Lost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Lost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lost_comet" title="Lost comet">Lost comet</a></div>
<p>A number of periodic comets discovered in earlier decades or previous centuries are now <a href="/wiki/Lost_comet" title="Lost comet">lost comets</a>. Their orbits were never known well enough to predict future appearances or the comets have disintegrated. However, occasionally a "new" comet is discovered, and calculation of its orbit shows it to be an old "lost" comet. An example is Comet <a href="/wiki/11P/Tempel%E2%80%93Swift%E2%80%93LINEAR" title="11P/Tempel–Swift–LINEAR">11P/Tempel–Swift–LINEAR</a>, discovered in 1869 but unobservable after 1908 because of perturbations by Jupiter. It was not found again until accidentally rediscovered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LINEAR" title="LINEAR">LINEAR</a> in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kronk_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-kronk-227">[225]</a></sup> There are at least 18 comets that fit this category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer-228">[226]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comets_in_fiction" title="Comets in fiction">Comets in fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Impact_events_in_fiction" title="Category:Impact events in fiction">Category:Impact events in fiction</a></div>
<p>The depiction of comets in <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a> is firmly rooted in the long Western tradition of seeing comets as harbingers of doom and as omens of world-altering change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_29_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_29-229">[227]</a></sup> Halley's Comet alone has caused a slew of sensationalist publications of all sorts at each of its reappearances. It was especially noted that the birth and death of some notable persons coincided with separate appearances of the comet, such as with writers <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> (who correctly speculated that he'd "go out with the comet" in 1910)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_29_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_29-229">[227]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eudora_Welty" title="Eudora Welty">Eudora Welty</a>, to whose life <a href="/wiki/Mary_Chapin_Carpenter" title="Mary Chapin Carpenter">Mary Chapin Carpenter</a> dedicated the song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halley_Came_to_Jackson" title="Halley Came to Jackson">Halley Came to Jackson</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_29_229-2"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_29-229">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>In times past, bright comets often inspired panic and hysteria in the general population, being thought of as bad omens. More recently, during the passage of Halley's Comet in 1910, Earth passed through the comet's tail, and erroneous newspaper reports inspired a fear that <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a> in the tail might poison millions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup> whereas the appearance of Comet Hale–Bopp in 1997 triggered the mass suicide of the <a href="/wiki/Heaven%27s_Gate_(religious_group)" title="Heaven's Gate (religious group)">Heaven's Gate</a> cult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact of comets</a> has been depicted as a threat overcome by technology and heroism (as in the 1998 films <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(film)" title="Deep Impact (film)">Deep Impact</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Armageddon_(1998_film)" title="Armageddon (1998 film)">Armageddon</a></i>), or as a trigger of global apocalypse (<i><a href="/wiki/Lucifer%27s_Hammer" title="Lucifer's Hammer">Lucifer's Hammer</a></i>, 1979) or zombies (<i><a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Comet" title="Night of the Comet">Night of the Comet</a></i>, 1984).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_29_229-3"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_29-229">[227]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Off_on_a_Comet" title="Off on a Comet">Off on a Comet</a></i> a group of people are stranded on a comet orbiting the Sun, while a large crewed space expedition visits Halley's Comet in Sir <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/2061:_Odyssey_Three" title="2061: Odyssey Three">2061: Odyssey Three</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Literature">In Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: In Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The long-period comet first recorded by Pons in Florence on 15th July 1825 inspired <a href="/wiki/Lydia_Sigourney" title="Lydia Sigourney">Lydia Sigourney</a>'s humorous poem <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg"><img alt="Wikisource-logo.svg" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Poems_Sigourney_1827/The_Comet_of_1825" title="s:Poems Sigourney 1827/The Comet of 1825">The Comet of 1825</a>. in which all the celestial bodies argue over the comet's appearance and purpose.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-169">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"I do not think that a comet is just a sudden fire, but that it is among the eternal works of nature." (<a href="#CITEREFSaganDruyan1997">Sagan &amp; Druyan 1997</a>, p. 26)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Seneca is quoted as stating, "Why ... are we surprised that comets, such a rare spectacle in the universe, are not yet grasped by fixed laws and that their beginning and end are not known, when their return is at vast intervals? ... The time will come when diligent research over very long periods of time will bring to light things which now lie hidden."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199737–38_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199737–38-172">[171]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-SCI-20150731-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-SCI-20150731_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBibringTaylorAlexanderAuster2015">Bibring, J.-P.;  et al. (31 July 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.aac5116" rel="nofollow">"Philae's First Days on the Comet – Introduction to Special Issue"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>. <b>349</b> (6247): 493. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2015Sci...349..493B" rel="nofollow">2015Sci...349..493B</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.aac5116" rel="nofollow">10.1126/science.aac5116</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26228139" rel="nofollow">26228139</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Science&amp;rft.atitle=Philae%27s+First+Days+on+the+Comet+%E2%80%93+Introduction+to+Special+Issue&amp;rft.volume=349&amp;rft.issue=6247&amp;rft.pages=493&amp;rft.date=2015-07-31&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26228139&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1126%2Fscience.aac5116&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2015Sci...349..493B&amp;rft.aulast=Bibring&amp;rft.aufirst=J.-P.&amp;rft.au=Taylor%2C+M.G.G.T.&amp;rft.au=Alexander%2C+C.&amp;rft.au=Auster%2C+U.&amp;rft.au=Biele%2C+J.&amp;rft.au=Finzi%2C+A.+Ercoli&amp;rft.au=Goesmann%2C+F.&amp;rft.au=Klingehoefer%2C+G.&amp;rft.au=Kofman%2C+W.&amp;rft.au=Mottola%2C+S.&amp;rft.au=Seidenstiker%2C+K.J.&amp;rft.au=Spohn%2C+T.&amp;rft.au=Wright%2C+I.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1126%252Fscience.aac5116&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComet"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-mass-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-mass_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
Halley: Using the <a href="/wiki/Volume#Formulas" title="Volume">volume of an ellipsoid</a> of 15×8×8 km * a <a href="/wiki/Rubble_pile" title="Rubble pile">rubble pile</a> density of 0.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup> yields a mass (m=d*v) of 3.02E+14 kg.<br/>
Tempel 1: Using a spherical diameter of 6.25 km; <a href="/wiki/Volume#Formulas" title="Volume">volume of a sphere</a> * a density of 0.62 g/cm<sup>3</sup> yields a mass of 7.9E+13 kg.<br/>
19P/Borrelly: Using the <a href="/wiki/Volume#Formulas" title="Volume">volume of an ellipsoid</a> of 8x4x4km * a density of 0.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup> yields a mass of 2.0E+13 kg.<br/>
81P/Wild: Using the <a href="/wiki/Volume#Formulas" title="Volume">volume of an ellipsoid</a> of 5.5x4.0x3.3 km * a density of 0.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup> yields a mass of 2.28E+13 kg.</span>
</li>

"Medical astrology in Spain during the seventeenth century". <i>Cronos (Valencia, Spain)</i>. <b>9</b>: 59–84. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1139-711X" rel="nofollow">1139-711X</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18543450" rel="nofollow">18543450</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Cronos+%28Valencia%2C+Spain%29&amp;rft.atitle=Medical+astrology+in+Spain+during+the+seventeenth+century&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.pages=59-84&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F18543450&amp;rft.issn=1139-711X&amp;rft.aulast=Lanuza+Navarro&amp;rft.aufirst=Tayra+M.+C.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComet"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-186"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-186">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFNewton1687"><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton, Isaac</a> (1687). "Lib. 3, Prop. 41". <a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica"><i>Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London" title="Royal Society of London">Royal Society of London</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-07647-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-07647-1"><bdi>0-521-07647-1</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Lib.+3%2C+Prop.+41&amp;rft.btitle=Philosophi%C3%A6+Naturalis+Principia+Mathematica&amp;rft.pub=Royal+Society+of+London&amp;rft.date=1687&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-07647-1&amp;rft.aulast=Newton&amp;rft.aufirst=Isaac&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComet"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-187"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-187">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSaganDruyan1997">Sagan &amp; Druyan 1997</a>, pp. 306–307</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Halley-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Halley_188-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHalleio1704">Halleio, E. (1704). <a class="external text" href="http://rstl.royalsocietypublishing.org/content/24/289-304/1882.full.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Astronomiae Cometicae Synopsis, Autore Edmundo Halleio apud Oxonienses. Geometriae Professore Saviliano, &amp; Reg. Soc. S"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London</i>. <b>24</b> (289–304): 1882–1899. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1704RSPT...24.1882H" rel="nofollow">1704RSPT...24.1882H</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1098%2Frstl.1704.0064" rel="nofollow">10.1098/rstl.1704.0064</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:186209887" rel="nofollow">186209887</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Philosophical+Transactions+of+the+Royal+Society+of+London&amp;rft.atitle=Astronomiae+Cometicae+Synopsis%2C+Autore+Edmundo+Halleio+apud+Oxonienses.+Geometriae+Professore+Saviliano%2C+%26+Reg.+Soc.+S&amp;rft.volume=24&amp;rft.issue=289%E2%80%93304&amp;rft.pages=1882-1899&amp;rft.date=1704&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A186209887%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frstl.1704.0064&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1704RSPT...24.1882H&amp;rft.aulast=Halleio&amp;rft.aufirst=E.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Frstl.royalsocietypublishing.org%2Fcontent%2F24%2F289-304%2F1882.full.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComet"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 1758 November 14, Alexis Clairaut announced to the Royal Academy of Sciences in Paris his prediction of the date at which Halley's comet would return:
<ul><li>Clairaut (January 1759) <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=ucm.5324346563;view=1up;seq=44" rel="nofollow">"Mémoire sur la cométe de 1682,"</a> <i>Le Journal des Sçavans</i>, pp. 38–45. On p. 44, Clairaut predicts that Halley's comet would return in mid April 1759. From <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=ucm.5324346563;view=1up;seq=50" rel="nofollow">p. 44</a> (translated from French): "  ... it seems to me that the expected comet must pass its perihelion towards the middle of next April." On p. 40, Clairaut states that his prediction might be slightly incorrect due to the presence of unknown planets beyond Saturn: "A body [i.e., Halley's comet] which passes into regions so remote, and which escapes our eyes during such long intervals, might be subjected to totally unknown forces; such as the action of other comets, or even of some planet always too far from the sun to ever be perceived."</li></ul>
On 1759 April 7, the French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Nicolas_Delisle" title="Joseph-Nicolas Delisle">Joseph-Nicolas Delisle</a> announced to the Royal Academy of Sciences in Paris that he and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Charles_Messier" title="Charles Messier">Charles Messier</a> had observed the return of Halley's comet, as predicted:
<ul><li>de l'Isle (June 1759) <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=ucm.5324346563;view=1up;seq=360" rel="nofollow">"Lettre de M. de l'Isle ... contenant la découverte du retour de la Comète de 1682, ... "</a> (Letter from Mr. de l'Isle ... containing the discovery of the return of the comet of 1682), <i>Le Journal des Sçavans</i>, pp. 356–364.</li></ul>
De l'Isle subsequently admitted that the comet's return had first been seen by a German amateur astronomer and farmer, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Palitzsch" title="Johann Georg Palitzsch">Georg Palitzsch</a>:
<ul><li>de l'Isle (August 1759) <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=ucm.5324346563;view=1up;seq=527" rel="nofollow">"Seconde lettre de M. de l'Isle,"</a> <i>Le Journal des Sçavans</i>, pp. 523–529. From p. 526 (translated from French): "  ... I received a letter from Heidelberg on the first of April in the evening, in which it is written to me that there had been published at Leipzig on the 24th of January of this year a German memoir in which it is said that this comet had been seen in Saxony by a peasant, named Palisch, on the 25th and 26th of December of last year; I can hardly conceive how this peasant could have discovered it, this comet ... "</li></ul>
The story behind the rediscovery of Halley's comet was given by Joseph Lalande in: 
<ul><li>Delalande, <i>Tables astronomiques de M. Halley, ... Et l'Histoire de la Comete de 1759.</i> [Astronomical tables of Mr. Halley, ... and the history of the comet of 1759.] (Paris, France: Durand, 1759), <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015006990892;view=1up;seq=105" rel="nofollow">pp. 91 ff.</a> Lalande acknowledged the contributions of Madame Lepaute to predicting the return of Halley's comet on p. 110. From <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015006990892;view=1up;seq=124" rel="nofollow">p. 110</a> (translated from French): " ... but it must be admitted that this immense series of details would have seemed frightening to me if <i>Madame LEPAUTE</i>, [who has] long applied [herself] successfully to astronomical calculations, had not shared in the work."</li></ul>
See also: 
<ul><li>Broughton, Peter (1985) "The first predicted return of comet Halley", <i>Journal for the History of Astronomy</i>, <b>16</b> : 123–132. Available at: <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/full/1985JHA....16..123B" rel="nofollow">Astrophysics Data System</a></li>
<li>Clairaut, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WQRbq4SA_zkC&amp;pg=PP7" rel="nofollow"><i>Théorie du mouvement des comètes,</i> ...</a> [Theory of the movement of comets, ...] (Paris, France: Michel Lambert, 1760); see especially the preface.</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199793-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaganDruyan199793_190-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSaganDruyan1997">Sagan &amp; Druyan 1997</a>, p. 93.</span>
</li>

Antonietta; Moreno, Raphael (2014). "Localized sources of water vapour on the dwarf planet (1) Ceres". <i>Nature</i>. <b>505</b> (7484): 525–527. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0028-0836" rel="nofollow">0028-0836</a>. <a class="mw-redirect"
